### 18 North Oak Development, Canada

This case study is provided courtesy of Creative Energy.

An example of an ambient temperature district energy system broke ground in Oakville, Ontario, Canada on March 28, 2023. Creative Energy developed and is implementing the ambient loop for the Minto Communities North Oak development. While this system serves a new construction development, it provides an example for implementing district ambient loops.

The North Oak project interconnects phase-by-phase geothermal fields to provide reliable space heating for five residential buildings developed over three phases. The district system will distribute ambient water at a temperature between 34°F and 90°F for space heating and cooling. The district system also supplies higher-temperature heating water at 145°F for local DHW production from a central natural gas boiler. As seen in the schematic diagram, each building is connected to the district system via two heat exchangers, one for space heating and cooling and one for DHW production. The ambient distribution network will interconnect three geothermal fields, which will be constructed in tandem with the building development schedule. The geothermal field for the first phase will consist of 70 boreholes drilled 600 feet deep. As each phase comes online, another field will be added to the system, increasing the low-carbon thermal energy capacity.

The combination of the geothermal fields and ambient distribution will allow buildings to share energy with one another and enable in-suite water-source heat pumps within the buildings to operate reliably and efficiently. The district system supplies all space conditioning needs using the geothermal ambient loop, with the boiler plant reserved for domestic hot-water heating and as a backup for the geothermal field. The district system is expected to decrease carbon emissions related to space heating by up to 95 percent and is expected to avoid almost 1000 tons of GHG emissions annually.

**[Figure: Schematic Diagram of North Oak Ambient District Loop]**

*Image content: A diagram showing the layout of the North Oak Ambient District Loop, including the geothermal fields, heat exchangers, and distribution network. The diagram also shows the expected carbon emissions reduction and GHG emissions avoidance.*

Figure CS18.1 Schematic Diagram of North Oak Ambient District Loop (Image credit: Creative Energy [2022])


# Chapter 6: Ventilation and Infiltration

## 6.1 Ventilation

Ventilation is the intentional introduction of outdoor air into a building to improve indoor air quality and reduce the concentration of indoor air pollutants. Ventilation can be achieved through various means, including:

* Natural ventilation: using windows, doors, and other openings to allow outdoor air to enter the building
* Mechanical ventilation: using fans and other mechanical systems to bring outdoor air into the building

### 6.1.1 Natural Ventilation

Natural ventilation is a cost-effective and energy-efficient way to ventilate buildings. It can be achieved through various means, including:

* Windows: opening windows to allow outdoor air to enter the building
* Doors: opening doors to allow outdoor air to enter the building
* Ventilation shafts: using ventilation shafts to bring outdoor air into the building

### 6.1.2 Mechanical Ventilation

Mechanical ventilation is a more effective way to ventilate buildings, especially in areas with high levels of indoor air pollution. It can be achieved through various means, including:

* Fans: using fans to bring outdoor air into the building
* Ventilation systems: using ventilation systems to bring outdoor air into the building

## 6.2 Infiltration

Infiltration is the unintentional introduction of outdoor air into a building through gaps and cracks in the building envelope. Infiltration can be a significant source of indoor air pollution, especially in areas with high levels of outdoor air pollution.

### 6.2.1 Causes of Infiltration

Infiltration can be caused by various factors, including:

* Gaps and cracks in the building envelope: allowing outdoor air to enter the building through gaps and cracks in the building envelope
* Poor construction: poor construction practices can lead to gaps and cracks in the building envelope, allowing outdoor air to enter the building

### 6.2.2 Effects of Infiltration

Infiltration can have significant effects on indoor air quality, including:

* Increased levels of indoor air pollution: infiltration can bring outdoor air pollutants into the building, increasing the levels of indoor air pollution
* Reduced energy efficiency: infiltration can reduce the energy efficiency of the building, as outdoor air can enter the building and increase the load on the heating and cooling systems

## 6.3 Ventilation and Infiltration Rates

Ventilation and infiltration rates are important factors in determining the indoor air quality of a building. Ventilation rates refer to the amount of outdoor air that is intentionally introduced into the building, while infiltration rates refer to the amount of outdoor air that enters the building unintentionally.

### 6.3.1 Ventilation Rates

Ventilation rates can be measured in various ways, including:

* Air changes per hour (ACH): measuring the number of times the air in the building is replaced per hour
* Cubic feet per minute (CFM): measuring the volume of air that is introduced into the building per minute

### 6.3.2 Infiltration Rates

Infiltration rates can be measured in various ways, including:

* Air leakage rate (ALR): measuring the amount of air that leaks into the building per hour
* Infiltration rate (IR): measuring the amount of air that enters the building unintentionally per hour

**[Figure: Ventilation and Infiltration Rates]**

*Image content: A graph showing the relationship between ventilation rates and infiltration rates. The graph shows that as ventilation rates increase, infiltration rates decrease.*

## 6.4 Conclusion

Ventilation and infiltration are important factors in determining the indoor air quality of a building. Ventilation can be achieved through various means, including natural ventilation and mechanical ventilation. Infiltration can be caused by various factors, including gaps and cracks in the building envelope and poor construction practices. Ventilation and infiltration rates are important factors in determining the indoor air quality of a building, and can be measured in various ways.


# 19 Kitsilano Pool, Canada

This case study is provided courtesy of FirstLight Energy Solutions.

Kitsilano Pool, one of Vancouver's seasonal outdoor pools, is a popular swimming destination from late May until September annually. After upgrading its aged domestic hot-water system, it now utilizes a central heat pump with CO2 refrigerant. Selected as the inaugural site for this system, Kitsilano Pool serves as a testing ground before citywide implementation of central HPWHs in other municipal buildings.

**Figure CS19.1 Exterior view of Kitsilano Pool.**

| Project Name | Kitsilano Pool | Year Built | 1978 |
| Location | Vancouver, British Columbia (Canada) | Building Size | — |
| Project Phase | Implemented 2021 | Year Last Systems Upgrade | N/A |

**Key EEMs/ERMs**

*   Low-flow fixture conversion (2022)
*   Central Domestic Hot-Water Heat Pumps (2022)

**Domestic Hot Plant**

| Existing | Retrofit |
| --- | --- |
| Mid-efficiency 725 MBH atmospheric domestic hot-water boiler with two 120-gallon unfired hot-water tanks | 210 MBH CO2 air-source heat pump water heater with 500 gallons of DHW storage and an electric resistance swing tank |

**Existing Domestic Hot-Water System**

The existing domestic hot-water system comprised a single 725 MBH mid-efficiency gas boiler supplying two 120-gallon hot-water storage tanks in the pool's main mechanical room. The system functioned on stand-alone controls, supplying hot water to the pool deck and changing room showers. All showerheads were rated at 2.5 GPM.

**Load-Reduction Measure**


# Case Study 19: Kitsilano Pool, Canada

All showerheads were retrofitted with 1.5 GPM showerheads, reducing the peak DHW demand by approximately 40%. The new DHW plant was integrated with the facility BAS, allowing remote monitoring and control, which can also be utilized for demand-response management.

## DHW Plant Retrofit

The City of Vancouver chose this site to implement a central heat pump water heater using CO2 refrigerant as a proof-of-concept test project before implementing them on other municipally owned buildings. In addition to eliminating GHG emissions associated with domestic hot water, a secondary goal was to cool the boiler room in summer using the heat pump for maintenance personnel using the workstation located there.

The system was sized to facilitate 100% of the DHW load, eliminating the need for natural gas for DHW heating. It consists of a single-pass air-source CO2 heat pump, capable of recovering 233 gallons per hour at a COP of 3.8. The heat pump supplies DHW to two 250-gallon primary storage tanks connected in series to promote stratification. To ensure backup functionality, the second primary tank includes a 54-kW electric heater in case of heat pump failure. A third 250-gallon tank, equipped with an 18-kW electric heater, acts as a swing tank, preventing recirculation of DHW from affecting the primary storage tanks' temperature stratification. Adherence to local building codes required the use of a double-wall heat exchanger with leak detection, preventing the use of the heat pump from directly heating domestic water. Instead, a small hydronic circuit employs a brazed plate double-wall heat exchanger to deliver heat from the heat pump to the domestic hot-water system.

**[Figure: Schematic diagram of Kitsilano Pool domestic hot-water system]**

*Image content: A diagram showing the domestic hot-water system, including the heat pump, storage tanks, and heat exchangers. The diagram illustrates the flow of water and heat through the system, highlighting the use of a double-wall heat exchanger and a brazed plate double-wall heat exchanger.*


# 6.4.2.2.2 Building Decarbonization Retrofits for Commercial and Multifamily Buildings

The system is equipped with a large number of temperature sensors to facilitate troubleshooting and characterize system operation. Used as a proof of concept, the city has since implemented similar central HPWH systems in other city-owned buildings. Monitoring of the system operation over the past two summers has indicated that the system has a lot of extra capacity. The next project on-site involves expanding the DHW system to heat pool makeup water and reduce GHG emissions from the natural gas boilers used to heat the pool.

**[Figure: DHW System Schematic]**

*Image content: A diagram showing the DHW system components, including the heat pump, storage tanks, and distribution pipes. The diagram also shows the connection to the pool makeup water system.*

The system is designed to provide a reliable and efficient source of hot water for the building's occupants, while also reducing greenhouse gas emissions from the natural gas boilers. The expansion of the system to heat pool makeup water will further reduce the building's carbon footprint and provide a cost-effective solution for the city.


# Chapter 7: Ventilation and Indoor Air Quality

## 7.1 Ventilation Requirements

The ventilation system shall be designed to provide a minimum of 10 cfm (4.7 L/s) of outdoor air per person for all occupied spaces. The system shall also be capable of providing a minimum of 20 cfm (9.4 L/s) of outdoor air per person for all occupied spaces during peak occupancy periods.

### 7.1.1 Ventilation Rates

The ventilation rates for various spaces are as follows:

| Space | Ventilation Rate (cfm/person) |
| --- | --- |
| Office | 10 |
| Classroom | 15 |
| Laboratory | 20 |
| Gymnasium | 25 |

### 7.1.2 Ventilation System Design

The ventilation system shall be designed to provide a minimum of 10 air changes per hour (ACH) for all occupied spaces. The system shall also be capable of providing a minimum of 20 ACH for all occupied spaces during peak occupancy periods.

## 7.2 Indoor Air Quality

The indoor air quality shall be maintained at a level that is safe and healthy for occupants. The system shall be designed to remove pollutants and contaminants from the air, and to provide a comfortable and healthy indoor environment.

### 7.2.1 Indoor Air Quality Standards

The indoor air quality standards for various spaces are as follows:

| Space | Indoor Air Quality Standard |
| --- | --- |
| Office | ASHRAE Standard 62.1-2019 |
| Classroom | ASHRAE Standard 62.1-2019 |
| Laboratory | ASHRAE Standard 62.1-2019 |
| Gymnasium | ASHRAE Standard 62.1-2019 |

### 7.2.2 Indoor Air Quality Monitoring

The indoor air quality shall be monitored regularly to ensure that it meets the standards set forth in Section 7.2.1. The monitoring shall include measurements of temperature, humidity, and pollutant levels.

**[Figure: Ventilation System Design]**


Image content: The figure shows a diagram of a ventilation system design, including the location of air intakes, exhaust fans, and ductwork. The diagram also shows the location of sensors and monitors for indoor air quality.


The ventilation system shall be designed to provide a minimum of 10 air changes per hour (ACH) for all occupied spaces. The system shall also be capable of providing a minimum of 20 ACH for all occupied spaces during peak occupancy periods.

## 7.3 Ventilation System Maintenance

The ventilation system shall be maintained regularly to ensure that it operates efficiently and effectively. The maintenance shall include cleaning of air filters, replacement of worn-out parts, and inspection of ductwork and vents.

### 7.3.1 Ventilation System Maintenance Schedule

The ventilation system maintenance schedule shall be as follows:

| Task | Frequency |
| --- | --- |
| Cleaning of air filters | Monthly |
| Replacement of worn-out parts | Quarterly |
| Inspection of ductwork and vents | Annually |

### 7.3.2 Ventilation System Maintenance Records

The ventilation system maintenance records shall be kept for a minimum of 5 years. The records shall include the date and time of maintenance, the tasks performed, and the results of any inspections or tests.

**[Figure: Ventilation System Maintenance Schedule]**


Image content: The figure shows a table of the ventilation system maintenance schedule, including the tasks to be performed, the frequency of performance, and the responsible person.


The ventilation system maintenance records shall be kept for a minimum of 5 years. The records shall include the date and time of maintenance, the tasks performed, and the results of any inspections or tests.

**Understanding Commercial and Multifamily Building Decarbonization Retrofits**

Decarbonizing existing buildings is a crucial step toward reducing global greenhouse gas emissions and combating climate change. Building Decarbonization Retrofits for Commercial and Multifamily Buildings is an essential resource for professionals and decision-makers in the building industry, offering clear pathways to decarbonizing existing commercial and multifamily properties.

This guide is organized to take the reader on a path through a decarbonization project, including goal setting, planning, identifying strategies, implementation, metric tracking, financing, and specific considerations for existing buildings. The guide details the best practices for developing and conducting decarbonization retrofits for commercial and multifamily buildings.

Key features of this guide include:

*   19 case studies showcasing diverse building types, systems, and decarbonization approaches
*   Actionable steps for each phase of a decarbonization project, from goal setting to implementation
*   Practical guidance on retrofitting HVAC systems, optimizing building envelopes, and utilizing renewable energy technologies
*   Expert recommendations on monitoring and tracking progress to ensure long-term success

By embracing these strategies, building owners and operators can not only reduce carbon emissions but also lower operating costs, improve occupant health, and contribute to a more sustainable future.
